He resigned when he was appointed director-general, but the relationship continued. We found attendance records for private functions at Rothwell House through 2023. And an email from two months ago — from Menon's personal account to the same private address — referencing 'the Kerala matter.'"

"So Rothwell knew about the repository before any of us."

"Rothwell suspected. Menon confirmed." Rathore guided them around a stone bench where an elderly woman was feeding pigeons. "The director-general was positioned to get the assignment to Thiruvananthapuram. He volunteered, according to his personnel file. Told the PM's office that his archaeological expertise was essential. What he really wanted was access."

Meera was quiet for a long time. They walked past the banyan trees, past the yoga group, past a stone fountain that hadn't worked in years.

"There's something else," she said. "Kesavan gave me the priest's log last night."

Rathore's stride didn't falter, but his head turned slightly.

"The priest's log?"

"Eighty generations of guardianship records. Every interaction between the temple and the outside world. It's handwritten — the earliest entries are on palm leaf. The most recent is from two days ago."

She told him about the dual cycle — that the mechanism would activate twice on alignment day, and that if no one directed the second cycle, the repository would open by default. The details were in the priest's log.

Rathore stopped walking. He stood on the wet grass, his tracksuit damp at the shoulders from the dripping banyan overhead, and stared at her.

"You're telling me that on the day the court orders Vault B opened, the mechanism will give whoever is present a choice: share the knowledge carefully, or open the floodgates."

"Yes."

"And the government doesn't know this."

"The government knows the mechanism exists. They know about Reveal because I demonstrated it. They don't know about Open, and they don't know about the default."

Rathore resumed walking, faster now. Meera matched his pace.

"This changes everything," he said. "If Rothwell gets to the mechanism on the alignment day, he can use the Open configuration to empty the repository. If the government gets there first, they'll try to seal it. If no one gets there—"

"Then it opens by default, and whoever is standing in the passage takes what they want."

They reached the far end of the park. The temple gopuram rose above the trees, its carvings dark with rain. Somewhere beneath it, the repository waited.

"We have ten days," Meera said.

"We have less than that. Rothwell's ICJ petition will force the government to respond within seventy-two hours. Once the legal process starts, everything accelerates. The government will want to control the narrative. Rothwell will want to control the access. And the mechanism will wait for none of them."

Meera touched the priest's log, wrapped in cotton, inside the cloth bag she carried over her shoulder. Eighty generations. 2,300 years. And it came down to ten days, a mechanism, and a choice that no court order could dictate.

"What do we do about Menon?" she asked.

Rathore's face went still. "That's the problem. If we expose him, the delegation collapses, the government is embarrassed, and the PM's office takes direct control of the repository — no committee, no international access, pure classification. If we don't expose him, Rothwell has eyes inside the system."

"Either way, we lose."

"Either way." Rathore looked at the temple, then back at her. "Unless we find a third option."

---

# Chapter 40

The third option, when it came, was not elegant.

Rathore spent the afternoon in his prefab office, the monsoon hammering the roof like a percussion section, building a case against Dr. Menon that he couldn't use. The evidence was damning but usable only as a weapon — and a weapon that destroyed the wielder as thoroughly as the target.

He had:

One: the consulting fees from the Rothwell Centre, documented in UK Companies House records. Forty thousand pounds a year for three years. Not illegal — advisory boards paid well — but compromising for a man who now held the highest archaeological office in India.

Two: the attendance records for Rothwell House functions — a private dinner in 2022, a cultural heritage summit in 2023, a reception that included, according to the guest list Rathore obtained through a contact at the British High Commission, Yuki Tanaka. Menon and Rothwell's primary dealer in the same room, eighteen months before the repository was discovered.

Three: the email. The handwritten note scanned and sent to a private account, timed eleven hours after the delegation's descent into the repository. The content was specific enough to constitute an intelligence leak under the Official Secrets Act.

And four: the email from two months ago, sent from Menon's personal account to the same private address, referencing "the Kerala matter." Two months ago was before Meera's arrival, before the GPR scans, before any of the official activity. Which meant Menon had been discussing the temple with Rothwell's people before the operation even began.

Rathore laid the four pieces of evidence on his desk and stared at them.

If he gave this to Director Shah, Menon would be arrested. The delegation would collapse. The PM's office would scramble for damage control, and the repository would become a political crisis rather than a cultural one. International press would have a field day: Indian official caught leaking to British billionaire. The colonial optics would be catastrophic.

If he gave it to Meera, she could use it as leverage — threaten to expose Menon unless the government included her in the decision-making process. But Meera had no official standing. She was a foreign agent who'd been dismissed. Her leverage was the dead-man's switch and the priest's log, neither of which she could deploy without consequences.

If he gave it to no one, Menon continued feeding information to Rothwell, and Rothwell continued outmaneuvering everyone.

Rathore picked up his phone and called Meera. She answered on the first ring.

"I need to show you something. Can you get to the park again?"

"Give me twenty minutes."

He gathered the evidence into a folder, tucked it under his arm, and walked out of the prefab into the rain. The monsoon didn't care about his problems. The monsoon had been falling for millions of years before anyone built a temple here, and it would fall for millions of years after the temple was dust.

Meera was waiting under the banyan tree where the yoga group usually gathered. She looked like she hadn't slept — her hair was pulled back in a messy ponytail, her kurta was rumpled, and her reading glasses were perched on her forehead, slightly crooked. She had the cloth bag with the priest's log over her shoulder.

Rathore handed her the folder. She opened it and read standing up, the rain drumming on the leaves overhead, drops falling onto the paper.

When she finished, she looked at him.

"Suresh Menon," she said. "Sixty-one years old. Three decades in the ASI. Published scholar. And he sold the repository's secrets to Edmund Rothwell for access to the manuscripts."

"Not for money," Rathore said. "For access. Menon is a genuine scholar. He wants to study the manuscripts. Rothwell promised him first access — the chance to publish, to translate, to put his name on the most significant archaeological discovery in Indian history. That's the currency that bought him."

"And in exchange, Rothwell gets real-time intelligence on the government's plans for the repository."

"And the legal standing to file an international petition with specific, verifiable details about the chamber's contents. Details that make his case credible. Details that came from the director-general of the Archaeological Survey of India."

Meera closed the folder. "If we expose him—"

"The government collapses the committee idea. The PM takes direct control. The repository becomes a national security asset. No international access. No scholarly oversight. Just the RAW director deciding what the world gets to know about Ashoka's legacy."

"And if we don't expose him—"

"Rothwell has eyes inside the system until the alignment day. He'll know every move the government makes. He'll position himself to be at the mechanism when it activates. He'll use the Open configuration and take everything."

Meera leaned against the banyan tree's trunk. The bark was rough and wet. She closed her eyes.

"There has to be a third option."

"I've been looking for one since 6 AM."

She opened her eyes. "What if we don't expose him — what if we use him?"

Rathore looked at her.

"Menon wants access to the manuscripts. Rothwell promised him access. What if we give him access — real access, committee-level access — in exchange for becoming our double agent? He feeds Rothwell controlled information. Misinformation, if we need it. And we watch what Rothwell does with it."

Rathore turned the idea over in his mind. It was risky. It was morally gray. It was exactly the kind of operation RAW trained its officers to execute.

"He'd have to agree to it."

"Menon is a scholar who made a deal with a billionaire because he wanted to study manuscripts. If we offer him legitimate access — the chance to publish, to translate, to be part of the official process — he has no reason to stay loyal to Rothwell."

"And if he refuses?"

"Then we expose him." Meera's tone was clinical. "But we do it strategically. We use the exposure to force the government into forming the international committee, instead of letting the PM's office take direct control. We make the scandal about the system, not about one man."

Rathore stared at her. She was standing in the rain under a banyan tree in Kerala, proposing a double-agent operation against one of the most powerful men in Indian archaeology, using leverage that included a 2,300-year-old priest's log and a dead-man's switch full of encrypted photographs. She had no official standing, no weapons, and a flight to New York in thirty-six hours.

She was, he realized, exactly the right person for this.

"I'll approach Menon tonight," Rathore said. "Quietly. Off the record. I'll lay out what we know and what we're offering. If he agrees, we have a channel into Rothwell's plans. If he doesn't—"

"Then tomorrow is going to be a very interesting day."

---

# Chapter 41

At 8 PM, Edmund Rothwell's legal team filed the petition.

At 10 PM, the story broke.

Meera watched both events from her hotel room, lying on the bed with her phone six inches from her face, scrolling through the cascade of notifications with the particular numbness of someone who knew this was coming and was still unprepared for the speed.

The petition — *International Cultural Heritage Preservation Trust v. Republic of India* — was filed with the International Court of Justice in The Hague at 3:30 PM local time. Yuki Tanaka's team had worked through the night, and the document was 147 pages long, citing eleven international conventions, six precedents, and — Meera felt her stomach drop when she reached paragraph thirty-four — a detailed description of the mechanism, the repository, and its contents.

The description was too specific. Too accurate. The number of copper cylinders (247). The number of stone tablets (89). The Ashoka Chronicle on its stone pedestal. The Brahmi-Indus translation key. These were details that only someone inside the chamber could have provided.

Menon. The director-general had delivered.

Rathore's approach must have failed. Or not happened yet. Or happened and been rebuffed. Meera sent a text: *The petition is live. Paragraph 34 is from our friend.* No response. She hadn't expected one — Rathore was inside the temple compound now, under government orders, his communications monitored.

The media story hit at 10 PM Indian time, published simultaneously on the International Herald Tribune's website and the front page of the London Times' digital edition. The headline:

**SECRET CHAMBER BENEATH INDIAN TEMPLE HOLDS ANCIENT KNOWLEDGE GOVERNMENT WON'T SHARE**

*By Robert Collins, Senior Cultural Affairs Correspondent*

The story was a masterwork of strategic journalism. It led with the Padmanabhaswamy Temple's existing fame — the trillion-dollar treasure, the Supreme Court case, the sealed vault — and then pivoted to the new discovery: a hidden chamber beneath the vaults containing "manuscripts of extraordinary scientific significance, including mathematical theorems predating European discoveries by centuries and documents in the undeciphered Indus Valley script."

The sourcing was careful. "A former cultural heritage official with direct knowledge of the site." "International legal experts." "Scholars familiar with the temple's history." None of these sources were named, but all of them pointed in one direction: the Indian government had found something extraordinary and was keeping it secret.

The framing was even more careful. The story didn't accuse India of wrongdoing — it simply asked questions. Why had the government classified the findings? What was in the chamber? Did the international community have a right to know about knowledge that could "reshape our understanding of human civilization"? The questions were rhetorical weapons, designed to make the government's silence look like guilt.

By 10:30 PM, the story was the top trending topic on Twitter in India, the UK, and the United States. By 11 PM, it had been picked up by the BBC, Al Jazeera, CNN, and every major Indian news outlet. By 11:30 PM, Meera's own phone was buzzing with messages from FBI colleagues who'd seen the story and were connecting it to her assignment.

She lay on the bed and watched it unfold. The feeling was strange — a mixture of admiration and disgust. Rothwell had done something she couldn't. He'd made the repository public. He'd forced the issue into the open. And he'd framed the narrative entirely on his own terms.

The Indian voices on Twitter were furious — not at Rothwell, but at the framing. "First they looted our temples, now they want our knowledge." "The British Museum has enough of our heritage." "This is the East India Company all over again." The colonial memory was raw, and Rothwell's petition — a British billionaire demanding access to Indian heritage through an international court — was the worst possible optics.

The Western voices were equally predictable. "Knowledge belongs to humanity." "India shouldn't hoard discoveries." "This is why we need international oversight." The colonial undertones were invisible to the people speaking them, which made them more offensive, not less.

Meera turned off her phone and lay in the dark, listening to the monsoon. The rain was constant, indifferent, ancient. It had been falling on this city long before Ashoka's engineers carved their mechanism into the bedrock. It would be falling long after the repository was opened, catalogued, and argued over by people who thought they owned something that had never belonged to anyone.

She thought about the priest's log, wrapped in cotton in her cloth bag. She thought about Kesavan's words: *"The knowledge will survive because it was designed to survive."* She thought about the dual cycle, and the choice that would be made in nine days by whoever stood at the mechanism when the sun entered the sanctum.

She thought about her father, who'd spent thirty years protecting a secret that was now the world's worst-kept mystery.

And she thought about Edmund Rothwell, who had just won the first round without firing a shot.

---

# Chapter 42

The morning was a fire.

Meera woke at 5 AM to the sound of her phone buzzing incessantly. She'd forgotten to turn it back on after midnight, and the notifications had accumulated like floodwater behind a dam — 247 of them, a number she noticed with dark amusement because it matched the count of copper cylinders in the repository.

The story had gone viral overnight. Not in the casual social-media sense — in the geopolitical sense. The Indian government had been forced to issue a statement at 3 AM, confirming the existence of "an archaeological site of significance" beneath the Padmanabhaswamy Temple while flatly denying that any knowledge was being "withheld from the international community." The statement's language was careful but its tone was furious — the particular anger of a government that had been caught off-guard.

By 6 AM, the PM's office had scheduled a press conference for 10 AM. By 7 AM, the opposition parties had demanded a parliamentary inquiry. By 8 AM, the hashtag #VaultB was trending in forty-seven countries.

Meera watched it all from her hotel room, sitting cross-legged on the bed with her laptop open, flipping between news sites with the grim focus of someone studying an opponent's opening moves.

The Indian media was split. The Times of India ran a patriotic headline: **INDIA'S GREATEST ARCHAEOLOGICAL DISCOVERY — GOVERNMENT MOVES TO PROTECT NATIONAL HERITAGE.** The Hindu was more measured: **HIDDEN CHAMBER BENEATH PADMANABHASWAMY TEMPLE RAISES QUESTIONS ABOUT ACCESS AND OWNERSHIP.** NDTV led with a panel discussion featuring three shouting academics and a moderator who'd given up trying to control them.

The international coverage was worse. The New York Times ran the story above the fold: **ANCIENT KNOWLEDGE FOUND IN INDIA — GOVERNMENT REFUSES INTERNATIONAL ACCESS.** The Guardian's headline was almost satirical in its British understatement: **CALLS FOR TRANSPARENCY AFTER INDIAN TEMPLE DISCOVERY.** Le Monde asked whether the discovery "challenged the Eurocentric narrative of scientific history."

Social media was a war zone. The two sides had crystallized overnight into mutually hostile camps:

Camp One: "Knowledge belongs to humanity. India has no right to hide this from the world. International scholars should have access immediately."

Camp Two: "This is India's heritage. Colonial powers looted our artifacts for centuries. We will decide who sees it and when."

Both sides were right. Both sides were wrong. Meera scrolled through the arguments and felt a growing disgust — not at the positions, which were predictable, but at the colonial undertones that infected both. Camp One couldn't see that their "knowledge belongs to humanity" argument was the same justification the British had used to take the Koh-i-Noor. Camp Two couldn't see that "India's heritage" included knowledge that could revolutionize medicine, computing, and materials science — knowledge that might save lives, if shared.

The PM's press conference began at 10 AM. Meera watched it on her laptop, the buffer stuttering on the hotel's unreliable WiFi.

The Prime Minister stood at a podium in Delhi, flanked by the Minister of Culture and the ASI director-general — Dr. Menon, looking distinctly uncomfortable, his eyes fixed on a point somewhere above the camera.

The PM's statement was a masterpiece of political triangulation. He claimed the discovery as a triumph of Indian heritage — "the knowledge of our ancestors, preserved for millennia, now revealed to the world." He emphasized India's commitment to "responsible stewardship" and "international scholarly cooperation." He deplored the "irresponsible media coverage" that had "distorted the nature of the findings for sensational purposes."

Then, at the end, the pivot:

"In recognition of the international significance of this discovery, the Government of India will form a limited international committee to advise on access to and study of the archaeological site. The committee will include distinguished scholars from India and abroad, representing the world's leading institutions. The committee's recommendations will guide the government's decisions on classification and access."

A reporter shouted a question: "Will the repository's contents be declassified?"

The PM smiled the smile of a man who'd answered harder questions from friendlier journalists. "The committee will advise. The government will decide."

Another reporter: "What about Edmund Rothwell's court petition?"

"The Government of India does not comment on pending legal proceedings. We are confident that any international tribunal will recognize India's sovereign right to protect and manage its own cultural heritage."

The press conference ended. Meera closed her laptop and sat in the silence of her hotel room.

The committee was a concession — a bow to international pressure. But the charter hadn't been written yet. The members hadn't been named. The terms of reference hadn't been defined. Everything was still to be decided, and Meera — who had discovered the repository, opened it, and documented its contents — was not part of any of it.

Her name was not mentioned. Her role was not acknowledged. She'd been written out of her own story.

She picked up her phone. Still no message from Rathore. The government had him locked down.

She picked up the priest's log. She opened it to Kesavan's final entry and read it again: *"The stones will yield again on that day. The mechanism will activate twice. Whoever is present will choose."*

Nine days. The mechanism would choose whoever was standing in front of it. And right now, the people positioning themselves to be there were the Indian government and Edmund Rothwell.

Not Meera Desai. Not the priest. Not the scholars who could actually understand what was in the chamber.

She set down the log and stared at the wall.

---

# Chapter 43

Meera spent the afternoon doing something she'd never done as an FBI agent: she thought about surrender.

Not actual surrender — strategic surrender. The kind of capitulation that looked like defeat but was actually a repositioning. The kind of losing that opened a door.

She had leverage. The photographs. The priest's log. The dead-man's switch. The Reveal equation. She had all the cards, and none of the chips. She could publish everything and watch the knowledge get weaponized. She could release nothing and watch the government and Rothwell fight over the mechanism. She could fly home tomorrow and pretend this had never happened.

None of these options led to the outcome she wanted: the knowledge preserved, protected, and shared on terms that honored both its significance and its fragility.

She was lying on the bed, staring at the ceiling, when her phone buzzed. She picked it up and saw the message she'd been ignoring for two days:

*Ms. Desai. We should talk. — E.R.*

She'd deleted the message twice. It had reappeared both times — not the same message, but new ones, sent from different numbers. Rothwell was persistent.

She stared at the latest one and felt something shift in her thinking. Rothwell had done something she couldn't. He'd made the repository public. He'd forced the issue. He'd created the conditions under which an international committee was being formed — a committee that, whatever its flaws, would provide a framework for access.

What if she could work with that? Not with Rothwell — she didn't trust him, and she wasn't wrong not to. But with the framework he'd created. The committee needed expertise. It needed someone who understood the mechanism, the repository, the manuscripts. It needed someone who had actually been inside the chamber.

It needed her.

But the government wouldn't appoint her. She was American, FBI, and had been explicitly dismissed. The PM's office wouldn't bring back a consultant they'd just fired.

Rothwell, on the other hand, had a seat at the table. He'd forced the committee into existence. He had the legal standing, the media connections, and the money to shape its composition. If he wanted Meera Desai on the committee, he could make it happen.

The thought made her skin crawl. Aligning with Edmund Rothwell — the man who'd spent decades acquiring Indian heritage for his private collection, whose family had profited from colonial looting for two centuries, who'd compromised the ASI director-general to get inside information — felt like making a deal with the devil.

But the devil had something she needed: access.

She sat up on the bed. She thought about her FBI training — specifically, the undercover operations module, where the instructors had drilled a single principle into her head: the best way to beat your opponent is to get inside their operation. Not to destroy it from the outside, but to be so close that you can redirect it from within.

She picked up her phone and called the number on Rothwell's latest message.

He answered on the first ring.

"Ms. Desai. I've been waiting for this call." His voice was warm, cultured, precise — the voice of a man who conducted billion-dollar negotiations over afternoon tea. "Shall I send a car?"

"I'll meet you at your hotel. Thirty minutes."

"I look forward to it. Ask for the presidential suite. The staff know me."

She hung up and sat very still for a moment. Then she showered, changed into a clean white kurta and dark trousers, put her reading glasses in her pocket, and checked the priest's log — still in the cloth bag, still wrapped in cotton, still containing the most dangerous piece of information in the entire operation: the dual cycle.

She left the hotel and walked through the monsoon rain to the Taj Vivanta, where Edmund Rothwell was waiting in his presidential suite with a pot of Darjeeling tea and a smile that didn't reach his eyes.

---

# Chapter 44

The presidential suite of the Taj Vivanta was not designed for comfort. It was designed for impression — floor-to-ceiling windows overlooking the monsoon-drenched city, silk upholstery in muted golds and creams, a marble coffee table bearing a silver tea service that probably cost more than Meera's annual salary. The impression it made was: the person who stays here has more money than you will ever have.

Rothwell rose from a wingback chair when she entered. He was taller than she'd expected — six-one, maybe six-two — with the lean, maintained physique of a man who worked with a personal trainer and didn't enjoy it. His hair was silver, swept back from a high forehead. His eyes were pale blue, striking against deeply tanned skin. He wore a linen shirt, open at the collar, and pressed chinos. No shoes — leather loafers set neatly by the door.

He looked like a man who had nothing to prove. This, Meera knew, was the most dangerous kind of person.

"Ms. Desai." He extended his hand. His grip was firm, brief, professional. "Please, sit. Tea?"

"No, thank you."

He sat back down and poured his own tea — a deliberate unhurriedness that put the conversational pace entirely in his control. Meera recognized the technique. She'd seen it in interrogation rooms, except there the bad coffee was government-issue.

"I've been following your career with some interest," Rothwell said. "The Subhash Kapoor case. The Geneva Freeport investigation. The recovery of the Nizam's jewels from that private collection in Monaco. You've done impressive work for someone your age."

"You've been following my career, or you've been preparing for this meeting?"

A slight smile. "Both, if I'm honest. I make it a practice to understand the people I'm likely to negotiate with. And I've known since the Supreme Court ordered Vault B opened that the FBI would send someone. When I learned it was you — an Indian-American art-crime specialist who reads Brahmi — I took particular interest."

"How particular?"

"I read your doctoral dissertation. South Asian provenance and the colonial art market. Excellent work, if I may say so. Your argument about the structural relationship between colonial-era looting and modern auction-house provenance gaps was... persuasive."

Meera said nothing. She let the silence work. Rothwell was a man who liked to fill silences — she could see it in the way his eyes moved, calculating whether he'd said enough or too much.

"You're wondering why I asked you here," he said finally.

"I know why you asked me here. You want to know what I have, and you want to position yourself to benefit from it. The question is whether I have any reason to help you."

Rothwell set down his teacup. "Fair enough. Let me be direct." He leaned forward, his pale eyes fixed on hers. "I believe the repository beneath the Padmanabhaswamy Temple contains knowledge that belongs to the world, not to any single government. I believe the Indian government will classify it, weaponize it, and control its dissemination for political purposes. And I believe that you — the only person who has seen the repository's contents and understands the mechanism — are in a unique position to ensure that doesn't happen."

"That's a lovely speech. It doesn't explain why you've spent decades acquiring Indian artifacts for your private collection."

The slight smile again. "I acquire artifacts because I believe in preservation. The Rothwell Foundation has funded more conservation work in South Asia than any other private entity. The objects in my collection are studied, published, and accessible to scholars. Can the Indian government say the same about the contents of Vault A?"

"The contents of Vault A are a national treasure."

"They are. And they've been locked in a legal dispute for fifteen years while the gold sits in sealed rooms, unstudied, unpublished, inaccessible to the scholars who could learn from it. Is that preservation, Ms. Desai? Or is it hoarding?"

Meera felt the pull of the argument — the seductive logic of a man who was genuinely intelligent and genuinely wrong. Rothwell believed what he was saying. That was what made him dangerous.

"What do you want?" she asked.

"A seat on the committee the Prime Minister just announced. Legitimate, public, transparent. I'll bring funding, expertise, and international credibility. In exchange, I'll support whatever access framework the committee recommends — including Indian custodianship of the physical manuscripts."

"And the scanning equipment? The high-resolution copier your people are preparing?"

For the first time, Rothwell's composure flickered. A micro-expression — surprise, or the awareness that his preparations weren't as secret as he'd assumed.

"You're well-informed."

"I'm an FBI agent. It's my job to know things."

Rothwell studied her for a long moment. Then he leaned back and crossed one leg over the other, his manner shifting from negotiator to something more like appreciation.

"You're right, of course. I want copies. Not for private sale — the very idea is crude. I want the knowledge documented and preserved in a form that can't be destroyed by a single government's decision to seal a vault. The original manuscripts stay in India. But the knowledge — the mathematics, the metallurgy, the medical texts — it should be in multiple locations, accessible to scholars worldwide. Is that so terrible?"

"It's terrible if you're the one controlling access to the copies."

"Then don't let me control them. Put them in a distributed archive — the British Library, the Bibliothèque Nationale, the Library of Congress, the National Mission for Manuscripts in Delhi. I'll fund the digitization and walk away."

Meera considered this. It was a good offer — too good. Which meant it was a concession designed to secure something larger.

"What aren't you telling me?" she said.

Rothwell uncrossed his legs and leaned forward again. "I'm not telling you that I've been collecting Indian mathematical manuscripts for thirty years. My family has been doing it for two hundred. We have pieces in our collection that would make your FBI colleagues weep — palm-leaf manuscripts from Kerala, copper plates from Tamil Nadu, birch-bark fragments from Kashmir. Some were acquired legitimately. Some were not. I'm not proud of the latter."

This, Meera realized, was the real negotiation. Not the committee seat — the legacy.

"If I support your committee appointment," she said slowly, "and if the committee's work includes a review of international collections holding Indian manuscripts—"

"You'd have my full cooperation. Including voluntary repatriation of any objects the committee determines were acquired through coercion or theft."

Meera stared at him. He was offering to give back stolen artifacts — potentially worth tens of millions of dollars — in exchange for a seat on a committee and the respectability that came with it.

"Why?" she asked.

"Because my collection is meaningless if it's tainted. I want to be remembered as the man who preserved Indian knowledge, not the man who stole it. The committee gives me that legacy."

Meera stood. She needed to move — to break the spell of the suite, the tea, the pale blue eyes. She walked to the window and looked out at the monsoon. The city was a blur of water and gray light. Somewhere below, people were living their lives, unaware that two people in a presidential suite were negotiating the fate of 2,300 years of knowledge.

"I'll support your committee appointment," she said without turning around. "Publicly. But you will not have unsupervised access to the repository. You will not bring scanning equipment. You will not copy anything without committee approval. And if I find out you've been operating outside those boundaries, I will release everything I have — your connection to Menon, your acquisition records, the Geneva Freeport holdings — to every journalist and prosecutor I know."

"Understood."

She turned. Rothwell was standing now too, his teacup in hand, his expression one of genuine respect — or a very good approximation of it.

"One more thing," she said. "The committee needs a founding secretary. Someone who understands the repository, the mechanism, and the manuscripts. Someone who was inside the chamber."

"I'll recommend you."

"They won't appoint an American FBI agent."

"They will if I make it a condition of my participation. And if the international media is watching." He set down his teacup. "You have the knowledge, Ms. Desai. I have the leverage. Together, we're more effective than either of us is alone."

Meera walked to the door. She paused with her hand on the handle.

"You know I'm going to be watching you," she said.

"I'd expect nothing less."

She opened the door and stepped into the corridor. Behind her, Rothwell's voice followed her out:

"You know I'm going to be on that committee, Ms. Desai. One way or another."

She didn't respond. She knew he was right.

---

# Chapter 45

Meera walked back to her hotel through the monsoon, the rain soaking through her kurta in the first thirty seconds, and let the water wash over her like a penance.

She'd just made a deal with the man who represented everything she'd spent her career fighting against — a collector who treated cultural heritage as a commodity, whose family had profited from colonial-era looting, who'd compromised a senior Indian official to gain inside information on the repository. And she'd done it because the alternative — standing on the sidelines while the government and Rothwell fought over the mechanism — was worse.

The rain was warm, almost pleasant once you stopped fighting it. She passed a tea stall where three men huddled under an awning, watching her with the bemused expressions of people who'd seen a crazy woman walking through a monsoon downpour and decided not to interfere. She waved. One of them waved back.

She reached her hotel, toweled off, changed, and called Rathore.

He answered on the fourth ring. His voice was low and guarded — he was being monitored.

"I need to meet. Tonight. The usual place."

"The usual place is under government surveillance."

"Then find an unusual one. Bring Kesavan if you can. It's important."

A pause. She heard him breathing. "The temple's eastern garden. After the last prayer. There's a storage shed behind the tulsi plants. Gopalan will leave it unlocked."

She called Kesavan next, through Gopalan. The message came back: *The Thantri will be there.*

At 9 PM, she slipped through the hotel's service entrance and walked to the temple. The monsoon had eased to a steady drizzle. The streets were nearly empty. She entered through the east gate — the one the devotees used during festival days, currently unguarded because the government's security cordon was focused on the north and south approaches.

Rathore was waiting in the garden, standing in the dark between two flowering frangipani trees. He was wearing civilian clothes again — dark pants, dark kurta, looking like a man on his way to dinner who'd taken a wrong turn into espionage.

Kesavan emerged from the shadows near the shed, moving with the quiet certainty of someone who'd navigated this garden in darkness for fifty years. His white veshti was dry — he knew the temple's covered pathways better than the monks who maintained them.

"You met with Rothwell," Kesavan said. It wasn't a question.

News traveled fast in the temple complex. Or the priest had his own intelligence network.

"I made a deal," Meera said. "He gets a committee seat. I get the founding secretary position. Together, we force the government into a three-way custodianship that no single entity can control."

She laid out the proposal: the temple trust would hold cultural custodianship, responsible for the physical site and the spiritual legacy. The ASI would hold scientific custodianship, responsible for conservation and study. And an international scholarly committee would hold academic custodianship, responsible for publication, translation, and dissemination.

No single entity could classify, seal, or remove the manuscripts without the agreement of the other two.

Rothwell would have a seat on the committee — because his presence ensured Western engagement, and because his self-interest would keep the process honest. He wanted the knowledge published and preserved because published knowledge enhanced his reputation. The thing about Edmund Rothwell was that his greed and his idealism pointed in the same direction.

Kesavan listened with his eyes closed, the way he did when he was processing something that challenged his understanding. When she finished, he was silent for a long time.

"The guardianship has always been single-threaded," he said. "One Thantri, one chain. You're proposing to make it a braid — three strands woven together. If one breaks, the others hold."

"Yes."

"And Rothwell is one of the strands."

"Rothwell is the thread we can see. The government is the thread we can't. At least with Rothwell, we know what he wants."

Kesavan opened his eyes. "Your father would say the same thing."

"My father is the reason I'm here. He trusted Kesavan Nambudiri. I trust Kesavan Nambudiri. And Kesavan Nambudiri just handed me 2,300 years of guardianship records and told me to carry them forward. I'm not going to do that alone."

Rathore spoke for the first time. "I can advocate within RAW. Director Shah won't like it — he wants full control — but the international pressure from the ICJ petition gives us leverage. The PM's office can't afford to look like they're hiding something."

"Do it," Meera said. "Send the proposal tonight. Before the government writes the committee charter without us."

Rathore nodded and melted back into the shadows.

Kesavan remained. He looked at Meera with those ancient eyes, and she saw something in them she hadn't seen before: not trust, exactly, but recognition. The look of a man who'd found his successor.

"You've changed," he said. "When you came to my house ten days ago, you were an art-crime investigator on a security assessment. Now you're negotiating with billionaires and intelligence officers over the fate of ancient knowledge."

"I had good teachers."

He almost smiled. "Your father. Rathore. And Ashoka — who understood, 2,300 years ago, that knowledge preserved is knowledge weaponized. The only defense is to share it carefully."

He turned and walked into the darkness of the garden, his bare feet silent on the wet stone.

At 3 AM, the response came. Meera was still awake, lying on her bed in the dark, the phone screen the only light in the room.

*Proposal received. Under review. No action until legal proceedings resolved.*

She stared at the message. Translation: the government was using Rothwell's court petition as cover to maintain control. They'd acknowledge the proposal, delay action, and hope the legal process gave them time to consolidate their position.

She typed back: *Clock is ticking. Alignment day is in nine days. The mechanism will activate with or without a committee.*

No response.

---

# Chapter 46

The committee was announced at 9 AM on Day 13, in a press release so carefully worded that it managed to acknowledge the repository's existence without admitting that the government had tried to hide it.

The Padmanabhaswamy International Knowledge Committee. Twelve members. Six Indian, six international. Representatives from India, Britain, the United States, Japan, Germany, and the Vatican. Mandate: to advise the Government of India on the conservation, study, and dissemination of the archaeological findings beneath the Padmanabhaswamy Temple.

Meera read the press release three times before she found her own name. It was in paragraph nine, after the list of distinguished scholars and diplomats: *Dr. Meera Desai, FBI Art Crime Team, has been appointed founding secretary, responsible for coordinating the committee's operations and maintaining liaison with the Government of India.*

She'd been given a role. Not a powerful one — secretaries didn't vote, didn't set policy, didn't control access. But they saw everything. They organized the meetings, distributed the documents, and kept the records. The founding secretary of an international committee was, in effect, its institutional memory.

Rothwell's name was in paragraph six: *Mr. Edmund Rothwell III, Chairman of the Rothwell Foundation, has been appointed to the committee in recognition of his Foundation's contributions to cultural heritage preservation in South Asia.*

Meera allowed herself a thin smile. He'd gotten his seat. She'd gotten hers. The question was whether the charter gave either of them any real power.

She read the charter. It was twenty-three pages long, drafted by government lawyers with the particular talent for saying everything and committing to nothing.

The committee's authority was advisory only. It could recommend, but the government would decide. It could request access, but the government would grant or deny. It could publish findings, but only after government review.

And then, buried in paragraph seven, the poison pill:

*"The Government of India reserves the right to classify specific findings as matters of national security, removing them from committee jurisdiction. Such classifications are subject to review by the Prime Minister's office and are not appealable by the committee or its members."*

The government had given with one hand and taken with the other. They'd created a committee — transparent, international, respectable — and then written themselves a loophole large enough to drive a military research program through.

Meera set down the charter and stared at the wall of her hotel room. The monsoon was building again outside, the rain hammering the window in waves.

She picked up her phone and called Rathore.

"Did you read paragraph seven?"

"Yes."

"They can classify anything they want. The mathematical manuscripts. The metallurgical formulae. The Indus Valley documents. Anything they deem a 'national security matter' disappears from committee oversight."

"I know."

"Was this Shah's doing?"

"Shah doesn't have the political weight for this. It's the PM's office. Mrs. Kapoor. She wrote the charter herself — I confirmed it through a contact in the Ministry of Culture."

Meera pressed her palm against her forehead. "So we have a committee that can look but not touch, advise but not decide, and publish only what the government approves."

"We have a seat at the table. That's more than we had yesterday."

"A seat at a table where the other side holds all the cards."

"Then we find ways to deal from the bottom of the deck." Rathore's voice was tight but steady. "The committee meets for the first time at 2 PM. Be there. Read the room. Find out who's with us and who's against us."

Meera hung up and opened her laptop. She had three hours before the first meeting. She used them to do what she did best: research.

By 2 PM, she had dossiers on all twelve committee members. Three were straightforward appointments — distinguished scholars with no political agenda. Two were Indian government loyalists who would vote however the PM's office directed. One — the Vatican representative, Father Thomas Kurien — was a Kerala-born paleographer whose involvement suggested the Vatican's interest in the repository extended beyond academic curiosity.

And then there was Rothwell, who would sit in the meeting with the calm of a man who'd already won, quietly calculating his next move.

---

# Chapter 47

Rothwell entered the committee's first meeting at 2 PM, precisely on time, carrying a leather portfolio and the relaxed demeanor of a man attending a board meeting of a company he intended to acquire.

The meeting room was a converted office in the temple's administrative building — air-conditioned, arranged with a large central table, twelve chairs, and a digital display for document sharing. The monsoon was audible through the walls, a constant reminder that nature didn't care about international committees.

Rothwell took his seat — third from the left, facing the door, a position that let him see everyone who entered and exited — and surveyed the other members with the practiced eye of a man who'd been sizing up boardrooms for forty years.

The Indian contingent was predictable. Two government loyalists — Dr. Anand Krishnan from the Ministry of Culture and Mrs. Lakshmi Iyer from the External Affairs Ministry — would vote with the PM's office. Dr. Menon, the compromised ASI director-general, was trying to look calm and failing. Two legitimate scholars — a Sanskritist from JNU and a conservation specialist from the National Museum — were genuinely excited about the repository. And Rathore, who'd been added at the last minute as the security liaison, sat at the end of the table with the carefully blank face of an intelligence officer who was watching everyone else watch each other.

The international members were more interesting. Professor Hans Mueller from the University of Heidelberg was a mathematician who'd published on the transmission of Indian numerals to the Islamic world. Dr. Yuki Tanaka — Rothwell's own dealer, though no one on the committee knew this yet — was ostensibly representing Japan's National Institute for Cultural Heritage. Father Thomas Kurien from the Vatican Archive was quiet, observant, and had the particular stillness of a man trained to listen. And Dr. Sarah Bennett from the Smithsonian was a South Asian art historian who'd published a devastating critique of colonial-era museum acquisitions — a pointed choice by whoever had drafted the American appointment.

Rothwell noted the alliances forming in real time. Mueller would follow whoever gave him access to the mathematical manuscripts. Bennett was ideologically aligned with open access. Kurien was an unknown quantity — his Vatican role suggested institutional caution, but his Kerala origins suggested personal investment. And Tanaka was his.

The meeting was chaired by Dr. Krishnan, who opened with a statement about India's commitment to "responsible stewardship" and "international scholarly cooperation" — the same language from the PM's press conference, deployed with the same careful emptiness.

Meera, as secretary, sat at the side of the room with a laptop, taking minutes. Rothwell caught her eye once and gave the faintest nod — an acknowledgment of their arrangement, or a calculation about her usefulness. Possibly both.

The first item of business was access. The committee needed to see the repository — not photographs, not descriptions, but the actual chamber, the actual manuscripts. Dr. Krishnan deferred to "security considerations."

Mueller leaned forward, his round face flushed with the particular urgency of a scholar who'd been told he couldn't see something. "With respect, Dr. Krishnan, I have traveled six thousand kilometers to advise on manuscripts I'm not permitted to view. This is — how do you say — not productive. Not productive at all."

Bennett was more measured. "Dr. Mueller's frustration is warranted, but I'd frame it differently. The committee's authority derives from its ability to make informed recommendations. We can't inform what we can't see. I'd urge the chair to propose a timeline for access rather than an open-ended deferral."

Tanaka said nothing. She wrote a single line in her notebook, showed it to no one, and closed it. Her face was a mask of polite attentiveness that revealed nothing about what she'd written or what she was thinking.

The government loyalists supported deferral.

It was Rothwell who broke the deadlock.

"If I may," he said, his voice carrying the particular authority of a man accustomed to being listened to. "The committee's credibility depends on its ability to verify the findings it's charged with advising on. We cannot fulfill our mandate from photographs and government summaries. I propose we schedule the first official access for this evening, with appropriate security protocols, and limit the initial visit to two hours."

The proposal was reasonable, measured, and impossible to argue against without looking obstructionist. Dr. Krishnan, caught off-guard, looked at Menon. Menon looked at his hands.

"Is there a second?" Krishnan asked.

Bennett raised her hand. Mueller raised his. Kurien raised his.

The motion passed. First access: 6 PM this evening.

After the meeting, Rothwell walked out through the temple garden, his leather portfolio under his arm, and made a phone call that no one on the committee heard.

"Yuki," he said. "The committee has access to the repository beginning this evening. I need the scanning equipment in place by then. High-resolution. Every manuscript, every page."

A pause. Tanaka's voice, calm and efficient: "The equipment is already in Thiruvananthapuram. My people can have it operational within two hours."

"Good. If we can't take the knowledge out, we'll copy it. And copies, my dear Yuki, are just as valuable as originals when you're the only one who has them."

He hung up and walked through the monsoon rain to his waiting car, his linen shirt darkening with water, his pale eyes fixed on the temple gopuram rising above the trees.

---

# Chapter 48

The second descent into the repository was different from the first.

The first time — Meera's midnight expedition with Rathore and Priya — had been frantic, desperate, a race against Mehra's men and the mechanism's timer. This time, it was organized. Official. Bureaucratic.

Twelve committee members. Four security officers. Two conservation specialists. One FBI agent taking minutes. They descended through the priest's tunnel in pairs, Rathore leading, the glow of flashlights and headlamps illuminating the ancient carvings on the passage walls.

The tunnel air was different from the city above — cool, mineral, tasting of stone and centuries. After three weeks of Thiruvananthapuram's monsoon humidity, where every breath felt like drinking and every surface was slick with moisture, the dry underground air was a shock. Meera's skin prickled as the temperature dropped, the sweat drying on her neck for the first time since she'd arrived. Somewhere far above them — hundreds of feet of granite and laterite and temple stone — the monsoon was hammering the city, filling the streets, turning the Karamana River into a brown torrent, but down here the air was still and ancient and untouched by weather. The only sound was their footsteps and the faint hum of the LED panels that Rathore's team had set up ahead.

Meera went last. She'd seen the repository before — fourteen minutes that had changed her life. But she wasn't prepared for the way it looked with proper lighting.

Rathore's team had installed portable LED panels at the chamber's entrance. The light transformed the space. What had been a dark void was now a library — shelves carved into the bedrock, stretching back beyond the light's reach, each one holding copper cylinders, stone tablets, and cedar boxes arranged with the precision of a cataloguing system that predated the Library of Alexandria.

The Ashoka Chronicle sat on its pedestal in the center of the chamber, the copper plates gleaming under the LEDs as if they'd been polished yesterday. Twenty-three centuries, and the bronze hadn't corroded. Ashoka's engineers had known things about metallurgy that modern science was only beginning to understand.

The scholars reacted as scholars do. Mueller walked directly to the mathematical manuscripts, picked up a copper cylinder, and began reading the Brahmi label with the focus of a man who'd forgotten everyone else in the room. After a moment, he made a sound — a sharp intake of breath, almost a gasp — and turned to the room with the wild eyes of a man who'd just found something he'd spent his career looking for.

"The positional notation," he said, his accent thickening with excitement. "This is not Brahmagupta. This is *before* Brahmagupta. Centuries before. This rewrites everything we understand about the development of Indian numerals. Everything." He turned back to the cylinder, already lost again.

Bennett stood in front of a set of palm-leaf illustrations — anatomical drawings, she realized, medical texts showing surgical procedures — and her hand went to her mouth. She didn't speak for a long moment. When she did, her voice was careful, the way it always was when she was processing something that mattered.

"These surgical illustrations," she said, examining a palm leaf showing what appeared to be a rhinoplasty procedure. "Sushruta's techniques. But these are more detailed than any known version of the Samhita. If these are contemporaneous with the repository's construction — third century BCE — they predate the earliest known surgical texts by centuries." She paused. "This isn't just history. This is a correction to history."

Tanaka moved through the chamber differently from the others — not with Mueller's uncontrolled excitement or Bennett's measured assessment, but with a quiet, systematic efficiency. She stopped at each shelf, examined the labeling system, and made notes in a precise, small hand. She didn't gasp, didn't exclaim, didn't comment. She catalogued. When she reached the metallurgical tablets, she paused for exactly three seconds longer than she'd paused at any other shelf, then moved on. Meera noticed the pause. She also noticed that Tanaka's notebook was now full.

Father Kurien, the Vatican paleographer, crossed himself before descending the last step, a gesture so automatic and so genuine that Meera felt a sudden, unexpected wave of emotion.

And Dr. Menon, the compromised director-general, moved through the chamber with his eyes fixed on the manuscripts — not with scholarly awe, but with the calculating gaze of a man cataloguing assets. Meera watched his eyes track from the copper cylinders to the stone tablets to the Indus Valley documents and then, briefly, to Rothwell, who was standing near the entrance with the composed expression of a man who'd seen it all before.

The look lasted less than a second. But Meera caught it.

Two hours. The committee members moved through the chamber in a state of controlled ecstasy, examining, photographing, documenting. Priya — who'd been added to the conservation team over Menon's objections — was at the far wall, working with a magnifying loupe over the Brahmi-Indus bilingual document. Her face was inches from the palm leaf, her glasses pushed up on her forehead, her lips moving silently as she traced the symbols.

Meera was recording the session on her phone — committee procedure required documentation — when Rathore appeared at her elbow.

"I need to talk to you," he said quietly. "Outside."

They stepped into the passage, out of earshot of the others.

"Menon," Rathore said. "I've confirmed he's the leak. The handwritten note we intercepted — I had a handwriting analyst compare it with his published correspondence. Match. And the private email account it was sent to traces through three shell companies to a Rothwell Foundation subsidiary in Liechtenstein."

"So we have proof."

"We have enough to end his career. But if we expose him now, the committee collapses. The PM's office uses the scandal as justification for taking direct control. No international access. No oversight. Just Director Shah and the national security clause."

"What's the alternative?"

"We contain him. Feed him controlled information through his channel to Rothwell. Let him think he's still operating freely while we watch what Rothwell does with the intelligence. Turn the leak into a weapon."

Meera looked back toward the chamber. Through the passage, she could see the LED lights, hear the murmur of scholars discovering things that would change their fields forever.

"And if Rothwell realizes we're feeding Menon misinformation?"

"Then we lose the channel but keep the committee. Better than losing both."

---

# Chapter 49

The next three days blurred into a rhythm of cataloguing, translating, and arguing.

Meera established the committee's operations in a converted office building near the temple — twelve workstations, secure storage for the manuscripts that were being brought up for study, and a server that Rathore's people had hardened against external intrusion. The cataloguing proceeded in shifts: mornings in the repository, afternoons in the office, evenings writing reports that no one outside the committee would read because everything was still classified.

The discoveries accumulated like snowdrift.

Mathematical manuscripts first. Mueller, working with Priya and a team of Brahmi specialists, identified forty-seven copper cylinders containing mathematical theorems. Twelve were commentaries on known works — Brahmagupta, Aryabhata, the Kerala School. But thirty-five were original. Theorems on infinite series that predated the Kerala School by centuries. A positional notation system more sophisticated than anything in the historical record. And a framework for what Mueller called "structured factorization" — a systematic approach to breaking down large numbers into their prime components.

Metallurgy next. The stone tablets contained formulae — chemical compositions, temperature curves, processing sequences — for materials that modern science couldn't fully replicate. Wootz steel was there, documented in detail, including the specific crucible temperatures and the carbon nanotube formation process that a 2006 Nature study had only partially explained. But there were other materials too: a copper alloy that resisted corrosion (the same technology that had preserved the Ashoka Chronicle for 2,300 years), and a ceramic that appeared to have thermal properties exceeding modern engineering ceramics.

Astronomy. Palm-leaf tables showing planetary positions, eclipse predictions, and stellar coordinates calculated with a precision that wouldn't be matched until the European Renaissance. The tables included mathematical techniques that looked, to Mueller's trained eye, like early forms of calculus — developed independently, 800 years before Newton.

Medicine. Surgical procedures illustrated on palm-leaf manuscripts — rhinoplasty, cataract extraction, lithotomy — with anatomical drawings of extraordinary accuracy. Bennett, reviewing these with a medical historian from AIIMS, found descriptions of instruments and techniques that paralleled Sushruta's known works but with refinements that suggested a continuous tradition of surgical innovation spanning centuries.

And the Indus Valley documents.

Priya worked on these alone, in a corner of the office, surrounded by magnifying loupes, reference grammars, and the Brahmi-Indus bilingual key she'd found on her first visit.

The first night, she didn't sleep. She sat cross-legged on the office floor with the high-resolution photographs spread around her like a paper mandala, the bilingual key in the center, and she read.

Not translated. Read.

The feeling was impossible to describe to anyone who hadn't spent their career staring at a dead script, knowing the sounds were there, the grammar was there, the voices were there, but unable to reach them. It was like standing outside a room where a party was in full swing — music playing, laughter erupting, conversations flowing — and the door was locked, and the key was in your hand, and you just couldn't find the lock.

Now she'd found the lock.

The first symbol she deciphered with certainty was the fish. In Brahmi, the fish glyph represented the sound *mi*. In the Indus script, it appeared in clusters — *mi-na*, *mi-ka*, *mi-ra*. She traced the phonetic equivalents through the bilingual key, cross-referencing each against the known Dravidian root words. *Min* — fish. *Mīn* — star. The same word, two meanings, connected by the visual metaphor of glittering in dark water.

She was weeping again. She'd been weeping on and off for three hours. She didn't wipe her eyes because her hands were covered in nitrile gloves and she didn't want to smudge the photographs.

The second breakthrough came at 3 AM. The Indus script wasn't purely linguistic — it was hybrid. Language and mathematics woven together, the way Egyptian hieroglyphs combined phonetic and logographic elements. A symbol that meant "wall" in the linguistic layer also carried a numerical value in the mathematical layer — thickness, height, material stress coefficient. The Indus Valley civilization had written in two dimensions at once: words for humans, numbers for engineers.

By Day 8, she had a working grammar. By Day 12, she could read continuous passages.

The documents weren't literature or religion. They were engineering records. Construction specifications, material calculations, structural analysis. The Indus Valley civilization had been building something extraordinary — and they'd documented every calculation in a script that combined language and mathematics.

On the evening of Day 13, Priya walked into Meera's office and placed a single page on the desk. It was a translation — the first complete passage of Indus Valley text ever rendered into English.

"She built reservoirs," Priya said. Her voice was hoarse from not speaking for three days. "This passage describes the construction of a reservoir complex. The slope of the inlet channel. The porosity of the filter material. The capacity in cubic units. It's a construction manual, Meera. Four thousand years old. And it's precise enough to build from."

Meera picked up the page. The translation was clean, confident, annotated with alternative readings in brackets.

"She?"

"The engineer. The author. The Indus script has gender markers — Dravidian languages do. The author of this section uses feminine markers. A woman. A woman engineer, four thousand years ago, writing a construction manual that her civilization could read and follow."

Priya sat down. Her hands were still trembling. "I've been crying on and off for two weeks. I'm not embarrassed. I spent my whole career — my whole life — trying to hear them. And now I can. They're not mysterious. They're not alien. They're engineers. They build things. They solve problems. They write it down. They're exactly like us."

Meera looked at the translation again. A woman's voice, four thousand years old, speaking through stone and time and a script that had been locked for longer than most languages had existed.

The knowledge was moving into the world. Slowly, carefully, under committee oversight. Twelve scholars from six countries, working together in a converted office building in Thiruvananthapuram, deciphering the accumulated wisdom of a civilization that had been forgotten for two millennia.

On the evening of Day 15, as the committee was wrapping up for the night, Meera received a secure message from Rathore.

*The RAW director has requested a private meeting. He wants to discuss "specific items of national security interest" in the repository. He's coming for the mathematical manuscripts.*

Meera read the message twice. She knew what was coming. The national security clause. The mathematical theorems were too valuable to share — especially the factorization framework, which Mueller had described as "potentially transformative for computational mathematics."

The government was about to make its move.

---

# Chapter 50

Director Shah arrived at 10 AM on Day 16, flanked by two aides who carried aluminum cases and the particular expression of people who were authorized to classify things.

He asked for a private meeting with the committee's Indian members. Meera, as secretary, was invited to attend and take minutes. Rothwell, as an international member, was not.

The meeting was held in the committee's conference room, the monsoon rain drumming against the windows like a reminder that the world outside was bigger than any government's plans.

Shah got directly to the point. "The Government of India has reviewed the committee's preliminary findings. Certain items have been identified as matters of national security interest and will be reclassified under the Official Secrets Act, effective immediately."

He opened one of the aluminum cases and produced a list. Meera counted: seventeen items. Twelve mathematical manuscripts, three metallurgical formulae, and two astronomical tables.

"Specifically," Shah continued, "the mathematical manuscripts relating to number factorization, the metallurgical processes for corrosion-resistant alloys, and the astronomical tables containing satellite-coordinate precision data. These items will be transferred to a secure government facility for further study."

Mueller, who'd been invited as a technical consultant, looked like he'd been slapped. "The factorization manuscripts are the most significant mathematical discovery in centuries. You can't simply—"

"I can, Professor. And I am. The committee charter, paragraph seven, grants the government explicit authority to classify items of national security interest. This is that authority, being exercised."

Meera spoke. "Director Shah, the committee hasn't been given an opportunity to review the classification request. The charter requires—"

"The charter requires that the committee be informed of classifications. It does not require committee approval. You've been informed."

The vote, when it came, was a formality. The Indian members supported classification — they had no choice, and most of them agreed with it anyway. The international members opposed it. The deadlock was academic; the national security clause gave Shah the authority regardless of the vote.

Shah collected his list and his aluminum cases and left.

The meeting broke up in a mood of barely suppressed fury. Mueller stalked out without speaking. Bennett made a call — Meera assumed it was to the Smithsonian. Father Kurien sat very still, his hands folded, his expression unreadable.

Rothwell, who'd been waiting outside, caught Meera's eye as she left the building. He didn't say anything. He didn't have to. The government had just done exactly what he'd predicted — classified the most valuable knowledge and locked it away. His media narrative — India won't share — had just been proven right.

That evening, after the committee had dispersed, Priya appeared at Meera's hotel room door. She was carrying a manuscript — not a photocopy, the actual palm-leaf bundle, in a conservation sleeve — and her face was pale.

"I held this back from the main inventory," Priya said without preamble. "It wasn't in the Reveal documentation — I found it in a side chamber that the LED panels didn't reach. I didn't tell Mueller. I didn't tell anyone."

She set the manuscript on the bed and unwrapped the conservation sleeve.

"It's a factorization algorithm. Not the framework Mueller found — this is something different. A complete, explicit, step-by-step method for factoring large prime products." Priya's voice was barely above a whisper. "Meera, if this works, it breaks RSA encryption. Every bank. Every government. Every secure communication in the world. Everything that relies on public-key cryptography becomes transparent."

Meera stared at the palm-leaf manuscript, its Indus symbols and Brahmi annotations neatly arranged in columns, and felt the temperature of the room drop by ten degrees.

"This is why Ashoka sealed it," Priya said. "This is why the repository was hidden for 2,300 years. Not because the knowledge was valuable — because it was dangerous."

---

# Chapter 51

Meera sat on the bed with the palm-leaf manuscript in her lap and felt two millennia of accumulated wisdom pressing against her ribs like a second heartbeat.

The algorithm was beautiful. Even to her untrained eye, the mathematical notation was elegant — symbols arranged in a sequence that was clearly systematic, each step building on the previous one with the precision of a proof. It was the work of a mind — or a tradition of minds — that had spent centuries thinking about the properties of numbers, arriving at a conclusion that modern cryptographers had spent decades trying to avoid.

RSA encryption — the foundation of modern digital security — relied on a simple principle: multiplying two large prime numbers was easy, but factoring their product back into its components was computationally infeasible. The difficulty of factoring large numbers was the wall that protected every bank transfer, every encrypted message, every classified communication in the world.

This manuscript scaled that wall.

The connection was not as strange as it sounded. Ancient Indian mathematicians had been obsessed with the properties of numbers for millennia. Brahmagupta, in the seventh century, had developed the kuttaka — the "pulverizer" — a systematic method for solving linear indeterminate equations that was, in essence, an ancestor of modern number theory. Aryabhata had computed pi to four decimal places and proposed that the Earth rotated on its axis. The Kerala School, in the fourteenth century, had discovered infinite series expansions of trigonometric functions two centuries before Newton and Leibniz. These were not coincidences or curiosities. They were the product of a continuous tradition of mathematical inquiry that stretched back to the Vedic period — a tradition that Ashoka's engineers had access to and built upon.

The manuscript in Meera's lap was the culmination of that tradition applied to a specific problem: how to break a number into its prime factors efficiently. The ancient mathematicians had approached it not with computers but with insight — a deep, structural understanding of how numbers related to each other that modern mathematics, dependent on brute-force computation, had never needed to develop.

The irony was savage. Modern encryption existed because factoring large numbers was hard. Factoring large numbers was hard because modern mathematicians had never developed the intuitive methods that ancient Indian scholars had perfected — methods that had been sealed in a vault for 2,300 years, waiting for someone to find them.

If the algorithm worked — and Priya's preliminary analysis suggested it did — it would reduce the computational complexity of prime factorization from effectively impossible to manageable. Not trivial, but manageable. A process that would take modern supercomputers millions of years could be completed in days or hours.

Every RSA-encrypted system in the world would become transparent. Bank accounts. Military communications. Diplomatic cables. Corporate secrets. Personal data. All of it, readable by anyone with access to the algorithm.

This was why Ashoka had sealed the repository. Not to hoard the knowledge — to protect the world from it.

Meera looked at Priya. The young scholar was sitting on the floor, her back against the wall, her rectangular glasses in her hand. She looked like she'd aged five years in five minutes.

"Does anyone else know about this?" Meera asked.

"Only you. I didn't tell Mueller. I didn't tell Rathore. I didn't put it in the inventory."

"Why?"

Priya pushed her glasses back onto her nose. The gesture was automatic, a habit that usually signaled she was about to say something analytical. This time, her voice was raw.

"Because I've spent my career studying ancient manuscripts. I've handled texts that were beautiful, texts that were historically significant, texts that made me cry." She paused. Her fingers found the edge of the conservation sleeve and gripped it, knuckles white. "In Lucknow, when I was starting my PhD, my supervisor told me that archaeology was about understanding the past, not changing the present. She said the most dangerous thing a scholar could do was confuse the two. I thought she was being dramatic."

She looked at the manuscript on the bed — the palm leaf that contained the most beautiful and most terrible mathematics she had ever encountered. The symbols were elegant. The logic was impeccable. The mind that had devised this algorithm, 2,300 years ago, had seen deeper into the structure of numbers than any modern mathematician. It was a work of genius. And it could end the world as she knew it.

The irony was not lost on her. She'd dreamed of finding something in an ancient manuscript that would change the world. She'd just never imagined the change might be catastrophic. The fifteen-year-old girl in the Lucknow library had wanted to decipher the past. The twenty-eight-year-old woman on the hotel room floor was holding the key to unlocking every secure communication on Earth.

"I have never held something that could hurt people," she said. "I needed to think before I decided what to do with it."

"And you decided to give it to me."

"You're the only person I trust to make the right decision. You're the only person who doesn't want anything from it."

Meera looked down at the manuscript. The palm leaf was cool and dry in her hands, the Brahmi numerals neat and precise, as if the scholar who'd written them knew exactly what they meant and had chosen to record them anyway.

The options paraded through her mind, each worse than the last.

Give it to the committee: It would be classified under the national security clause within hours. The algorithm would go to India's military research apparatus, which would weaponize it — not because the military was evil, but because that's what militaries did with powerful knowledge.

Give it to the government directly: Same result, faster timeline.

Publish it: Anyone with the mathematical skill to implement it could break encryption worldwide. Terrorists. Hostile governments. Criminal organizations. The knowledge would be free, and the world would pay the price.

Give it to Rothwell: He'd sell access to the highest bidder, framing it as "democratization of knowledge." The result would be the same as publication, except Rothwell would profit from it.

Destroy it: The only copy outside the repository. But the original was still in the chamber, and once the government catalogued the repository's contents — which they were doing right now — they'd find the algorithm anyway.

Keep it hidden: The original would be discovered. The government would classify it. And Meera would be holding a secret that could end her career, her freedom, and possibly her life.

Every path led to the same destination: the algorithm would be weaponized by someone. The only variable was who.

At 11 PM, her phone rang.

Unknown number. She almost didn't answer. Then she did.

"Ms. Desai. This is Mrs. Kapoor, from the Prime Minister's office."

The voice was calm, professional, and carried the particular authority of someone who spoke for the most powerful person in India.

"The Prime Minister has been informed of a specific manuscript in the repository — one that describes a factorization algorithm with significant security implications. We need to discuss it. You will bring the manuscript to Delhi tomorrow morning. A car will collect you from your hotel at six."

It was not a request.

"How did you know I had it?" Meera asked.

A pause. Then: "Ms. Desai. The committee's inventory records show a gap. Seventeen items were classified by Director Shah. One item — a palm-leaf manuscript from a side chamber — is not in the repository and not in the inventory. You are the founding secretary. We assumed you would understand the significance before anyone else."

"I haven't decided what to do with it."

"The Prime Minister has decided. It is a matter of national security. The car will arrive at six."

The line went dead.

Meera sat in the dark with the manuscript in her hands and the rain drumming on the window and the knowledge that the most powerful government in the world's largest democracy was coming for a 2,300-year-old palm-leaf document that could break the internet.

---

# Chapter 52

Meera did not get in the car.

At 6 AM, when the black sedan pulled up outside her hotel, she was already in the committee office, surrounded by files, the palm-leaf manuscript locked in a evidence bag in the office's single safe.

She sent a message to Mrs. Kapoor: *I am not traveling to Delhi. The algorithm manuscript is in the committee's custody. Any decision regarding its classification requires a full committee vote under paragraph seven of the charter, which specifies that the founding secretary must be present for any reclassification vote.*

The response came in four minutes: *The charter does not require your presence. It requires notification. You have been notified.*

Meera called Rathore. No answer. She called again. Still no answer.

She called his deputy, Anand.

"He's been recalled to Delhi," Anand said, his voice barely above a whisper. "They sent a car for him at five this morning. 'Consultation,' they said."

"That's not a consultation. That's a removal."

"I know. He told me to tell you: the national security clause is being invoked for the algorithm this afternoon. The vote is a formality. The Indian members have been instructed."

"Who's instructing them?"

"The PM's office. Mrs. Kapoor personally."

Meera leaned back in her chair. The committee office was empty at this hour — she was the only one there, surrounded by the detritus of three days of cataloguing: photocopied manuscripts, translation notes, empty coffee cups, the accumulated evidence of twelve scholars trying to understand 2,300 years of knowledge in three weeks.

She called Priya. "Where are you?"

"My hotel room. I haven't slept."

"Get to the office. Now. Bring everything you have on the algorithm — your translation notes, the Brahmi cross-references, all of it."

Priya arrived in twenty minutes, looking like she'd been through a washing machine. Her hair was loose and tangled, her glasses smudged, her kurta inside out. She was carrying a spiral notebook, a USB drive, and the hollow expression of someone who'd spent the night confronting the implications of her own expertise.

"The algorithm works," Priya said without preamble. "I ran the numbers. The theoretical framework is sound — a structural property of prime products that modern mathematics missed entirely."

"How long would it take to implement?"

"With modern computing? If you built a dedicated system using the algorithm's framework — months, maybe. A year at most. Once implemented, factoring a 2048-bit RSA key would take hours instead of millennia."
